Mod. TY, edited]<br /><a href="https://diariodeavisos.elespanol.com/2022/03/detectan-el-mosquito-aedes-aegypti-en-la-palma-sin-dengue-ni-zika/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://diariodeavisos.elespanol.com/2022/03/detectan-el-mosquito-aedes-aegypti-en-la-palma-sin-dengue-ni-zika/</a><br /><br /><br />The Canary\'s Entomological Surveillance System has detected 2 larvae of the _Aedes aegypti_ mosquito on La Palma Island, but this is "an isolated finding" and they do not have viruses that carry transmissible diseases, the Ministry of Health reported.<br /><br />_Aedes aegypti_, known as the yellow fever mosquito, "is a vector that transmits viral diseases in other geographic areas where these pathogens are endemic, which is not the case in the Canary Islands."<br /><br />The sequencing of the larvae has verified that they do not contain viruses that carry transmissible diseases, but in any case "we must also activate the detection and surveillance protocol."<br /><br />In 2017, the Entomological Surveillance System of the Canary Islands detected the presence of this mosquito in Fuerteventura Island, from where it was definitively eradicated (see Invasive mosquito - Spain: (CN) <a href="javascript:;" onclick="window.open(\'https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=20171214.5503487\');">20171214.5503487</a>).<br /><br />In this case, the presence of invasive mosquito larvae "has been detected in a specific area" of the island of La Palma, so residents will be surveyed and possible breeding sites inspected and located, as well as more traps will be placed in the area.<br /><br />The Ministry of Health points out that the surveillance system allows the early detection of invasive mosquito larvae and eggs, "before their presence has been reported or before they have caused bites in the island\'s population."<br /><br />"All the necessary information is being compiled to limit, verify and, where appropriate, eradicate the presence of this mosquito on the island," the Ministry states.<br /><br />The presence of the mosquito "does not mean that there is transmission of [of viruses that cause] diseases, such as dengue, Zika, yellow fever or chikungunya, since these [viruses] are not present in our territory, except for sporadic imported cases."<br /><br />The Surveillance and Public Health System team, coordinated by the General Directorate of Public Health in collaboration with the Directorate of the La Palma Health Area is carrying out initial preventive action.<br /><br />The placement of traps has been expanded at points considered to be at greatest risk, such as greenhouses, banana packing plants, cemeteries, and tire dumps, in addition to the port and airport, where they are currently located by protocol.<br /><br />_A. aegypti_ mosquitoes are black with [silver] stripes and smaller than the usual ones in the Canary Islands. They appear more in urban environments and have become adapted to reproduce in small water collection sites generated by people.<br /><br />They usually bite during the day and not at night and their bite generates a strong inflammatory reaction that is accompanied by a great irritation. They move nimbly close to the ground and no buzzing is heard.<br /><br />The female mosquitoes are the ones that bite, since they need to feed on blood to reproduce. They also need water as breeding sites to complete their development. They lay their eggs in the water, from which the larvae emerge, then transform into pupae (both are aquatic) and finally become adult mosquitoes.<br /><br />Mosquitoes that bite humans need to be breeding very close to people. That is why the most effective measure against them is to monitor or eliminate their breeding sites.<br /><br />--<br />Communicated by:<br />Mahmoud Orabi<br />&lt;ahstandards@gmail.com><br /><br />[ProMED-mail thanks Mahmoud Orabi for submitting this report.<br /><br />This is yet another example of movement of _Aedes aegypti_ to a new location. The possible mechanism of transport in this case is not mentioned. This is the 2nd report of the appearance of _A. aegypti_ in the Canary Islands. The 1st was in 2017. The entomological surveillance in the Islands has been very effective in detecting the presence of _A. aegypti_ very early on and eliminating them. The risk of new introductions of this mosquito is ongoing. One hopes that surveillance and response will continue to be successful in preventing the establishment of this mosquito or others in the Canary Islands. - Mod.TY] <h2>See Also</h2>\n2017<br />----<br />Invasive mosquito - Spain: (CN) <a href="javascript:;" onclick="window.open(\'https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=20171214.5503487\');">20171214.5503487</a><br />.................................................jh/ty/mj/jh</div>', 'zoom_lat': '28.000000', 'zoom_lon': '-15.500000', 'zoom_level': '5', 'markerInfoArr': 'Array\n(\n    [0] => Array\n        (\n            [place_id] => 203\n            [name] => Canary Islands [Spain]\n            [type] => l\n            [lat] => 28.000000\n            [lon] => -15.500000\n            [alert_id] => 8701924\n            [alert_tag_id] => \n            [dup_count] => 0\n            [summary] => PRO/AH/EDR> Invasive mosquito - Spain: (CN)\n            [display_date] => 11 Mar 2022\n            [disease_id] => 197\n            [score] => 2\n        )\n\n)\n', 'contents': {'203': ['11 Mar 2022 <a href="javascript:;" id="id8701924" class="bcl"> Invasive mosquito - Spain: (CN)</a>']}, 'markers': {'203': ['l1', 'Canary Islands [Spain]', '28.000000', '-15.500000']}}

'14321', 'reviewed': None, 'search_string_id': None, 'content': 'CORONAVIRUS DISEASE 2019 UPDATE (69): BLOOD GROUP, HOST GENETIC VARIATION, MENTAL DECLINE, DEXAMETHASONE, WHO\n*************************************************************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nIn this update:\r\n[1] Blood group\r\n[2] Host genetic variation\r\n[3] Mental decline\r\n[4] Dexamethasone\r\n[5] WHO: Daily new cases reported (as of 09 Mar 2022) \r\n[6] Global update: Worldometer accessed 09 Mar 2022 22:07 EST (GMT-5)\r\n\r\n******\r\n[1] Blood group\r\nDate: Wed 9 Mar 2022\r\nSource: Medical News Today [abridged, edited]\r\n<https://www.medicalnewstoday.com/articles/covid-19-severity-blood-group-may-influence-risk>\r\n\r\n\r\n- Recent research has identified a causal link between specific blood proteins and the risk of hospitalization, the need for respiratory support, or the risk of death from severe COVID-19.\r\n- While certain blood proteins were associated with an increased risk of severe COVID-19, others were protective.\r\n- Confirming the findings and exploring their clinical applicability will require further research.\r\n\r\nOften, people with severe COVID-19 require hospitalization with intensive care and ventilation. Although this has not been conclusively proven, scientists believe that an overactive and abnormal immune response to SARS-CoV-2 may be responsible for the damage seen in severe COVID-19. \r\n\r\nGenetics influence the response of the innate immune system. And so, genetic differences may help explain why some people are more likely to develop severe COVID-19.\r\n\r\nPrevious research has identified relationships between different genetic factors, hospitalization, and the need for ventilation due to COVID-19. [COVID-19 Host Genetics Initiative. Mapping the human genetic architecture of COVID-19. Nature 600, 472-477 (2021). <https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-021-03767-x>]\r\n\r\nOther studies have shown genetic variations between blood proteins modulating the immune response in healthy individuals.\r\n\r\nThis lead the authors of the new study to turn to data from genome-wide association studies. They looked for blood proteins associated with the risk of hospitalization, the need for respiratory support, and death from severe COVID-19. The team has published its results in the journalPLOS Genetics. [Palmos AB, Millischer V, Menon DK, et al., Proteome-wide Mendelian randomization identifies causal links between blood proteins and severe COVID-19. PLoS Genetics. Published: 3 March 2022 <https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1010042>]\r\n\r\nStudy co-author Dr. Alish Palmos, from the Social, Genetic, and Developmental Psychiatry Research Centre at King\'s College London, explained to Medical News Today that the researchers looked at thousands of proteins using Mendelian randomization.\r\n\r\n"Mendelian randomization uses genetic variants associated with a trait, i.e., protein level, and measures their causal effect on disease outcomes, [avoiding] environmental confounding factors, such as lifestyle, being physically ill, etc."\r\n\r\nAfter examining more than 3000 blood proteins using a genetic tool, they found 14 proteins with causal effects, including the:\r\n- blood group proteins ABO and KEL\r\n- antigen recognition proteins CD207 and SFTPD, structures on the surface of the virus identified by the immune system\r\n- adhesion molecules SELL, SELE, PECAM-1, and sICAM-1, which recruit virus-killing white blood cells called neutrophils to the site of inflammation\r\n- transporters RAB14 and ATP2A3, which are proteins that move materials across a cell membrane\r\n- enzymes GCNT4, C1GALT1C1, FAAH2, LCTL, which are proteins that speed up chemical reactions in a cell.\r\n\r\nThe researchers found that increased levels of ABO, C1GALT1C1, CD207, GCNT4, and RAB14 were associated with a 1.12 to 1.35 times greater risk of hospitalization, need for respiratory support, or death from COVID-19.\r\n\r\nHigher levels of FAAH2 indicated a 1.19 times increased risk of hospitalization only.\r\n\r\nHigher levels of the adhesion molecules PECAM-1, SELE, and SELL decreased the risk of hospitalization, need for respiratory support, or death from COVID-19 by 9-20%.\r\n\r\nIncreased ATP2A3, KEL, LCTL, and SFTPD were linked to decreased hospitalization risk, while higher levels of sICAM-1 were associated with a reduced risk of death or need for respiratory support.\r\n\r\nDr. Fady Youssef, a pulmonologist, internist, and critical care specialist at Memorial Care Long Beach Medical Center, provided context for MNT: "This study offers some [ideas] about ways that we can [...] better predict which patients will have a worse [...] course and which patients are [...] protected based on their genetics."\r\n\r\nDr. Palmos was surprised that the study did not show an association between "classic pro-inflammatory proteins," such as C-reactive protein or interleukin-6, and severe COVID-19. "It was also surprising to see such a consistent protective effect of adhesion molecules with severe COVID-19."\r\n\r\nThe strengths of the study included its design and use of Mendelian randomization. Weaknesses include the reliance on data from other studies, which could be subject to error despite quality control measures.\r\n\r\nDr. Palmos stated, "Clinical studies in patients would need to be carried out first to confirm our findings. One such study is the COVID-Clinical Neuroscience Study, which is investigating the causes behind different aspects of COVID-19." [<https://www.liverpool.ac.uk/covid-clinical-neuroscience-study/>]\r\n\r\nDr. Youssef told MNT, "Once you start applying [the data] widely, then you understand the [true] impact." He believes that this study "adds another piece in that puzzle [...] that would inform which interventions would be offered to patients depending on their risk profile."\r\n\r\n[Byline: Lori Uildriks]\r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nProMED\r\n\r\n******\r\n[2] Host genetic variation\r\nDate: Mon 7 Mar 2022\r\nSource: Nature [abridged, edited]\r\n<https://www.nature.com/articles/s41586-022-04576-6>\r\n\r\n\r\nCitation\r\nKousathanas, A., Pairo-Castineira, E., Rawlik, K. et al. Whole genome sequencing reveals host factors underlying critical COVID-19. Nature (2022). Published 7 Mar 2022, doi.org/10.1038/s41586-022-04576-6\r\n\r\nAbstract\r\nCritical COVID-19 is caused by immune-mediated inflammatory lung injury. Host genetic variation influences the development of illness requiring critical care or hospitalization following SARS-CoV-2 infection. The GenOMICC (Genetics of Mortality in Critical Care) study enables the comparison of genomes from critically-ill cases with population controls in order to find underlying disease mechanisms. Here, we use whole genome sequencing in 7491 critically-ill cases compared with 48 400 controls to discover and replicate 23 independent variants that significantly predispose to critical COVID-19. We identify 16 new independent associations, including variants within genes involved in interferon signalling (IL10RB, PLSCR1), leucocyte differentiation (BCL11A), and blood type antigen secretor status (FUT2). Using transcriptome-wide association and colocalisation to infer the effect of gene expression on disease severity, we find evidence implicating multiple genes, including reduced expression of a membrane flippase (ATP11A), and increased mucin expression (MUC1), in critical disease. Mendelian randomisation provides evidence in support of causal roles for myeloid cell adhesion molecules (SELE, ICAM5, CD209) and coagulation factor F8, all of which are potentially druggable targets. Our results are broadly consistent with a multi-component model of COVID-19 pathophysiology, in which at least 2 distinct mechanisms can predispose to life-threatening disease: failure to control viral replication, or an enhanced tendency towards pulmonary inflammation and intravascular coagulation. We show that comparison between critically-ill cases and population controls is highly efficient for detection of therapeutically-relevant mechanisms of disease.\r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nMary Marshall\r\n\r\n******\r\n[3] Mental decline\r\nDate: Wed 9 Mar 2022\r\nSource: CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease Research and Policy) [edited]\r\n<https://www.cidrap.umn.edu/news-perspective/2022/03/mental-decline-seen-older-covid-patients-1-year-later>\r\n\r\n\r\nMental decline seen in older COVID patients 1 year later\r\n--------------------------------------------------------\r\nCognitive impairment was more common among COVID-19 patients 60 years and older--particularly those with severe illness--released from hospitals in Wuhan, China, than among their uninfected peers, according to a 1-year follow-up study yesterday in JAMA Neurology.\r\n\r\nA team led by researchers at Daping Hospital in Chongqing, China, followed 1438 COVID-19 survivors aged 60 and older released from one of 3 dedicated COVID-19 hospitals in Wuhan from 10 Feb -- 10 Apr 2020, and compared them with 438 of their uninfected spouses.\r\n\r\nBecause pre-COVID cognitive status wasn\'t available, family members provided their perceptions of cognitive changes using the Chinese version of the short form of the Informant Questionnaire on Cognitive Decline in the Elderly (IQCODE). The questionnaire contains 16 items that rate changes in memory and other domains, with decline defined as an item score of 3.5 or higher. \r\n\r\nTrained raters interviewed COVID-19 patients at 12 months over the phone to evaluate cognition using the Chinese version of Telephone Interview of Cognitive Status-40 (TICS-40), which includes 10 variables and has a maximum of 40 points. A score of 20 or lower was considered indicative of mild cognitive impairment (MCI), while a score of 12 or lower indicated dementia, and a decrease of 3 points or more was considered clinically meaningful cognitive decline.\r\n\r\nOf the COVID-19 patients. 48.1% were men, median age was 69 years, and none had a history of dementia. The control group was 50.7% men, and the median age was 67 years.\r\n\r\nThe incidence of impaired cognition among COVID-19 survivors was 12.5% 12 months after hospital release. COVID-19 survivors had lower TICS-40 scores than controls at both 6 months (median, 29 vs 30 points) and 12 months (29 vs 31).\r\n\r\nSeverely ill patients had lower TICS-40 scores than those with milder illness and controls (median score, 24 vs 30 vs 31) at 6 months; at 12 months, they had lower scores than controls (22.5 vs 31). Patients with mild or moderate illness and controls differed in IQCODE scores but not TICS-40 over follow-up.\r\n\r\nAmong severely ill COVID-19 patients, 10% had dementia and 26.5% had MCI at 6 months. At 12 months, 15% had dementia and 26.2% had MCI, much higher than in those with milder illness (dementia, 0.8%; MCI, 5.4%) and controls (dementia, 0.7%; MCI, 5%). Survivors of mild or moderate COVID-19 and controls had similar incidences of dementia and MCI at both 6 and 12 months.\r\n\r\nSevere illness was tied to a higher risk of early-onset cognitive decline (odds ratio [OR], 4.9; 95% confidence interval [CI], 3.3 -- 7.2), late-onset cognitive decline (OR, 7.6; 95% CI, 3.6 -- 16), and progressive cognitive decline (OR, 19; 95% CI, 9.1 -- 39.5), while milder COVID-19 was associated with a higher risk of early-onset cognitive decline (OR, 1.7; 95% CI, 1.3 -- 2.3) after adjusting for age, sex, educational attainment, body mass index (BMI), and underlying illnesses.\r\n\r\nThe prevalence of progressive decline was 21.2% with severe COVID-19, 1.2% with non-severe COVID-19, and 2.3% in controls.\r\n\r\nSevere COVID-19 was linked to a higher risk of cognitive impairment at 12 months (OR, 9.1; 95% CI, 5.6 -- 14.8), but milder illness was not (OR, 1.1; 95% CI, 0.7 -- 1.8), after adjusting for age, sex, educational attainment, BMI, and underlying illnesses.\r\n\r\nAmong a subset of 438 patients and their spouses, COVID-19 survivors were older (median age, 68 vs 67 years) and had a higher incidence of hypertension (47% vs 34.5%). Both nonsevere (OR, 1.8; 95% CI, 1.1 -- 3) and severe COVID-19 (OR, 5.9; 95% CI, 3.6 -- 9.8) in this group were tied to a higher risk of cognitive impairment at 12 months, after adjustment for risk factors.\r\n\r\nThe study authors noted that impaired cognition is common in the months after COVID-19 infection, especially among older patients. "It is worth noting that 21% of individuals with severe cases in this cohort experienced progressive cognitive decline, suggesting that COVID-19 may cause long-lasting damage to cognition," they wrote.\r\n\r\n"These findings imply that the pandemic may substantially contribute to the world dementia burden in the future."\r\n\r\nPotential factors in post-COVID cognitive decline, the authors said, include long-term low oxygen levels, chronic systemic inflammation, and direct viral invasion of the brain and resultant neuronal damage.\r\n\r\n"In this cohort study of COVID-19 survivors 60 years and older who were discharged from COVID-19-designated hospitals in Wuhan, China, SARS-CoV-2 infection, especially severe infection, was associated with an increase in risk of longitudinal cognitive decline," the researchers concluded. "The findings highlight the importance of immediate measures to deal with this challenge."\r\n\r\n[Byline: Mary Van Beusekom]\r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nMary Marshall\r\n\r\n[Citation. Liun YH, Chen Y, Wang QH, et al. One-Year Trajectory of Cognitive Changes in Older Survivors of COVID-19 in Wuhan, China. A Longitudinal Cohort Study. JAMA Neurol. Published online 8 Mar 2022. doi:10.1001/jamaneurol.2022.0461 \r\n\r\nConclusions and relevance. In this cohort study, COVID-19 survival was associated with an increase in risk of longitudinal cognitive decline, highlighting the importance of immediate measures to deal with this challenge.\r\n\r\nMeaning. The findings suggest that long-term cognitive decline is common after SARS-CoV-2 infection, indicating the necessity of evaluating the impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on the future dementia burden worldwide. - Mod.LK]\r\n\r\n******\r\n[4] Dexamethasone\r\nDate: Wed 9 Mar 2022\r\nSource: CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease Research and Policy) [edited]\r\n<https://www.cidrap.umn.edu/news-perspective/2022/03/news-scan-mar-09-2022>\r\n\r\n\r\nContinuing dexamethasone after COVID hospital release shows no benefit\r\n----------------------------------------------------------\r\nContinuing the corticosteroid dexamethasone after hospital release didn\'t benefit COVID-19 patients who didn\'t complete the 10-day drug regimen during their stay, finds a study published yesterday [Tue 8 Mar 2022] in JAMA Network Open.\r\n\r\nKaiser Permanente Southern California researchers studied the continuing use of dexamethasone at a dosage of 6mg per day after hospital release in 1164 adult COVID-19 patients who were released from 1 of 15 hospitals from [1 May to 30 Sep 2020].\r\n\r\nAmong the 1164 patients, 59.5% continued dexamethasone after hospital release. Median patient age was 55 years, 70.6% were Hispanic, 57.9% were men, and 90% needed supplemental oxygen during hospitalization.\r\n\r\nThe rate of hospital readmission or death within 14 days of release was 9.1% in patients who continued dexamethasone, compared with 11.4% among patients who didn\'t, but the difference wasn\'t statistically significant.\r\n\r\nThe adjusted odds ratio (OR) for readmission or death within 14 days was 0.87 (95% confidence interval [CI], 0.58 -- 1.30) for patients who continued dexamethasone, compared with those who did not. A sensitivity analysis that limited the treatment group to those given exactly 10 days of dexamethasone showed comparable results (OR, 0.89; 95% CI, 0.55 -- 1.43).\r\n\r\nA subgroup analysis stratified by length of dexamethasone therapy in the hospital also yielded similar findings (1 to 3 days: OR, 0.71; 4 to 9 days: OR, 1.01), oxygen requirement at release (room air: OR, 0.91; supplemental oxygen use: OR, 0.76), and illness duration at release (10 days or less: OR, 0.81; more than 10 days: OR, 0.94).\r\n\r\nThe study authors noted that corticosteroids like dexamethasone were the first type of medication to show a survival benefit in severely ill COVID-19 patients and are now a mainstay of hospital treatment. Because early readmissions after hospital release are largely due to respiratory causes that probably reflect illness progression, the authors had theorized that continuing corticosteroids could reduce these outcomes.\r\n\r\n"Continuing treatment with dexamethasone, 6mg/d, at discharge was not associated with a reduction in 14-day all-cause readmission or mortality," the researchers wrote. "This finding suggests that dexamethasone should not be routinely prescribed beyond discharge."\r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nMary Marshall\r\n<mjm2020@googlemail.com>\r\n\r\n[Citation. Huang CW, Yu AS, Song H,et al. Association Between Dexamethasone Treatment After Hospital Discharge for Patients With COVID-19 Infection and Rates of Hospital Readmission and Mortality. JAMA Netw Open. 2022;5(3):e221455. doi:10.1001/jamanetworkopen.2022.1455\r\n\r\nIntroduction. Corticosteroids were the first medication class to demonstrate mortality benefit among patients with COVID-19 and are now a cornerstone of COVID-19 treatment. In the Randomized Evaluation of COVID-19 Therapy (RECOVERY) trial, the use of dexamethasone, 6 mg/d, up to 10 days or until discharge resulted in lower mortality. However, with no standardized discharge practices, corticosteroid prescription practices at discharge have varied.\r\n\r\nConclusions and relevance. In this cohort study of patients with COVID-19, continuing treatment with dexamethasone, 6mg/d, at discharge was not associated with a reduction in 14-day all-cause readmission or mortality. This finding suggests that dexamethasone should not be routinely prescribed beyond discharge for individuals with COVID-19. - Mod.LK]\r\n\r\n********\r\n[5] WHO: Daily new cases reported (as of 9 Mar 2022)\r\nDate: Wed 9 Mar 2022\r\nSource: WHO [abridged, edited]\r\n<https://covid19.who.int/table>\r\n\r\n\r\n*Daily case reports as of 9 Mar 2022 16:48 CET\r\n\r\nSurveillance\r\n------------\r\nWHO region (no. countries/territories):\r\nTotal confirmed cases (new cases in last 24 hours) / Total deaths (new deaths in last 24 hours)\r\n--------------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\nWestern Pacific Region (19): 29 262 597 (730 009) / 189 656 (892)\r\nEuropean Region (61): 184 618 493 (777 549) / 1 894 846 (2509)\r\nSouth East Asia Region (10): 56 285 919 (55 423) / 767 165 (533)\r\nEastern Mediterranean Region (22): 21 362 890 (14 737) / 337 357 (253)\r\nRegion of the Americas (54): 148 322 100 (125 821) / 2 652 042 (2289)\r\nAfrican Region (49): 8 460 530 (669) / 170 403 (11)\r\nCases on an international conveyance: 764 (0) / 13 (0)\r\n\r\nConfirmed cases (new cases in last 24 hours) / Total deaths (new deaths in last 24 hours)\r\nGrand total: 448 313 293 (1 704 208) / 6 011 482 (6487)\r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nProMED\r\n\r\n[Data by country, area, or territory for 9 Mar 2022 can be accessed at \r\n<https://promedmail.org/wp-content/uploads/world-pdf/2022%20WHO%20Data%20Tables%20MAR9_1646937489.pdf>.\r\n\r\n- The Americas region reported 7.3% of cases and 35.2% of deaths in the past 24 hours having reported more than 148.32 million cases, 2nd to the European region, as the most severely affected region. The USA reported 69 688 cases followed by Brazil and Chile. An additional 4 countries reported more than 1000 cases (Canada, Argentina, Guatemala, and Uruguay) in the past 24 hours. Additionally, Colombia and Costa Rica reported more than 500 but fewer than 1000 cases. \r\n\r\n- The European region reported 45.6% of cases and 38.6% of deaths over the last 24 hours, as the most affected region, with cumulative cases exceeding 184.61 million. Some countries reporting few or no cases in the last 24 hours or longer include Belgium (8 cases), Switzerland (22 cases), and Tajikistan, among others. A total of 35 countries reported more than 1000 cases in the past 24 hours, 1 country reporting more than 100 000 cases, 14 reporting more than 10 000, 17 reporting over 1000 cases, and an additional 3 countries reported more than 500 but fewer than 1000 cases.\r\n\r\n- The Eastern Mediterranean region reported 0.86% of daily case numbers and 3.9% of the deaths reported in the past 24 hours, having reported a cumulative total of more than 21.36 million cases. Iran (11 090) reported the highest number of cases over the last 24 hours followed by Bahrain, Tunisia, and Lebanon. Egypt and Iraq reported more than 500 but less than 1000 cases over the last 24 hours. \r\n\r\n- The African region reported only 669 cases and 11 deaths in the past 24 hours having reported a cumulative total of more than 8.46 million cases. DRC (117) reported the highest number of cases over the last 24 hours with most other countries reporting below 100 cases. A total of 33 countries did not report any cases over the last 24 hours. The low reported numbers are not a true reflection of the prevalent COVID19 situation in the region.\r\n\r\n- The Western Pacific region reported 42.8% of daily case numbers and 13.7% deaths in the past 24 hours, having reported a cumulative total of more than 29.26 million cases. South Korea (342 446) reported the highest number of cases over the last 24 hours followed by Vietnam, Japan, Australia, Malaysia, China, New Zealand, Singapore, Brunei, and Philippines.\r\n\r\n- The South East Asia region reported 3.2% of the daily newly reported cases and 8.2% of reported deaths in the past 24 hours, having reported a cumulative total of more than 56.28 million cases. Indonesia (26 336) reported the highest number of cases followed by Thailand (22 073), India (4575), Myanmar (1301), Sri Lanka (675) and Bhutan (386). \r\n\r\nOn the Overview tab at the WHO source URL, the epidemic curve of confirmed COVID-19 cases by WHO region, 30 Dec 2019 through 9 Mar 2022, is an excellent visual representation of the pandemic. - Mod.UBA]\r\n\r\n******\r\n[6] Global update: Worldometer accessed 9 Mar 2022 22:07 EST (GMT-5)\r\nDate: Wed 9 Mar 2022\r\nSource: Worldometer [edited]\r\n<https://www.worldometers.info/coronavirus/#countries>\r\n\r\n\r\nTotal number of reported deaths: 6 043 977\r\nTotal number of worldwide cases: 451 809 254\r\nNumber of newly confirmed cases in the past 24 hours: 1 812 295\r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nProMED\n\n[For those who wish to see the detailed global data, a snapshot of the Worldometer table at the time we accessed it is available at  <https://promedmail.org/wp-content/uploads/world-pdf/2022%20WORLDDATA%20MAR9_1646937515.pdf>.\r\n\r\nA 7-day series of cumulative data reported by countries, territories, and reporting entities can be found at <https://promedmail.org/wp-content/uploads/world-pdf/2022%20MAR9WORLD7_1646937529.pdf>. - Mod.UBA]\r\n\r\nIn the past 24 hours, 29 countries including South Korea (327 532), Vietnam (265 163), the Germany (191 973), France (69 190), Brazil (49 078), Russia (58 675), Netherlands (74 236), UK (66 870), Italy (49 815), Japan (62 747), USA (51 148), Austria (47 795), Switzerland (33 518), Turkey (33 243), Malaysia (30 246), Indonesia (26 336), Australia (26 098), Hong Kong (25 991), Greece (23 335), Thailand (22 984), Spain (21 936), Singapore (17 051), Chile (16 467), Denmark (15 992), Portugal (14 595), Poland (14 440), Tunisia (12 103), Slovakia (11 712), and Czech republic (10 527), reported the highest numbers of cases, all reporting more than 10 000 newly confirmed cases. Date:</span> 2022-03-11 13:33:18 AEDT <br /><span class="blue">Subject:</span> PRO/AH/EDR> COVID-19 update (69): blood grp., host genetic var., mental decline, dexam., WHO <br /><span class="blue">Archive Number:</span> 20220311.8701920<div class="text1">CORONAVIRUS DISEASE 2019 UPDATE (69): BLOOD GROUP, HOST GENETIC VARIATION, MENTAL DECLINE, DEXAMETHASONE, WHO<br />*************************************************************************************************************<br />A ProMED-mail post<br /><a href="http://www.promedmail.org" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">http://www.promedmail.org</a><br />ProMED-mail is a program of the<br />International Society for Infectious Diseases<br /><a href="http://www.isid.org" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">http://www.isid.org</a><br /><br />In this update:<br />[1] Blood group<br />[2] Host genetic variation<br />[3] Mental decline<br />[4] Dexamethasone<br />[5] WHO: Daily new cases reported (as of 09 Mar 2022) <br />[6] Global update: Worldometer accessed 09 Mar 2022 22:07 EST (GMT-5)<br /><br />******<br />[1] Blood group<br />Date: Wed 9 Mar 2022<br />Source: Medical News Today [abridged, edited]<br /><a href="https://www.medicalnewstoday.com/articles/covid-19-severity-blood-group-may-influence-risk" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.medicalnewstoday.com/articles/covid-19-severity-blood-group-may-influence-risk</a><br /><br /><br />- Recent research has identified a causal link between specific blood proteins and the risk of hospitalization, the need for respiratory support, or the risk of death from severe COVID-19.<br />- While certain blood proteins were associated with an increased risk of severe COVID-19, others were protective.<br />- Confirming the findings and exploring their clinical applicability will require further research.<br /><br />Often, people with severe COVID-19 require hospitalization with intensive care and ventilation. Although this has not been conclusively proven, scientists believe that an overactive and abnormal immune response to SARS-CoV-2 may be responsible for the damage seen in severe COVID-19. <br /><br />Genetics influence the response of the innate immune system. And so, genetic differences may help explain why some people are more likely to develop severe COVID-19.<br /><br />Previous research has identified relationships between different genetic factors, hospitalization, and the need for ventilation due to COVID-19. [COVID-19 Host Genetics Initiative. Mapping the human genetic architecture of COVID-19. Nature 600, 472-477 (2021). <a href="https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-021-03767-x" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-021-03767-x</a>]<br /><br />Other studies have shown genetic variations between blood proteins modulating the immune response in healthy individuals.<br /><br />This lead the authors of the new study to turn to data from genome-wide association studies. They looked for blood proteins associated with the risk of hospitalization, the need for respiratory support, and death from severe COVID-19. The team has published its results in the journalPLOS Genetics. [Palmos AB, Millischer V, Menon DK, et al., Proteome-wide Mendelian randomization identifies causal links between blood proteins and severe COVID-19. PLoS Genetics. Published: 3 March 2022 <a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1010042" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1010042</a>]<br /><br />Study co-author Dr. Alish Palmos, from the Social, Genetic, and Developmental Psychiatry Research Centre at King\'s College London, explained to Medical News Today that the researchers looked at thousands of proteins using Mendelian randomization.<br /><br />"Mendelian randomization uses genetic variants associated with a trait, i.e., protein level, and measures their causal effect on disease outcomes, [avoiding] environmental confounding factors, such as lifestyle, being physically ill, etc."<br /><br />After examining more than 3000 blood proteins using a genetic tool, they found 14 proteins with causal effects, including the:<br />- blood group proteins ABO and KEL<br />- antigen recognition proteins CD207 and SFTPD, structures on the surface of the virus identified by the immune system<br />- adhesion molecules SELL, SELE, PECAM-1, and sICAM-1, which recruit virus-killing white blood cells called neutrophils to the site of inflammation<br />- transporters RAB14 and ATP2A3, which are proteins that move materials across a cell membrane<br />- enzymes GCNT4, C1GALT1C1, FAAH2, LCTL, which are proteins that speed up chemical reactions in a cell.<br /><br />The researchers found that increased levels of ABO, C1GALT1C1, CD207, GCNT4, and RAB14 were associated with a 1.12 to 1.35 times greater risk of hospitalization, need for respiratory support, or death from COVID-19.<br /><br />Higher levels of FAAH2 indicated a 1.19 times increased risk of hospitalization only.<br /><br />Higher levels of the adhesion molecules PECAM-1, SELE, and SELL decreased the risk of hospitalization, need for respiratory support, or death from COVID-19 by 9-20%.<br /><br />Increased ATP2A3, KEL, LCTL, and SFTPD were linked to decreased hospitalization risk, while higher levels of sICAM-1 were associated with a reduced risk of death or need for respiratory support.<br /><br />Dr. Fady Youssef, a pulmonologist, internist, and critical care specialist at Memorial Care Long Beach Medical Center, provided context for MNT: "This study offers some [ideas] about ways that we can [...] better predict which patients will have a worse [...] course and which patients are [...] protected based on their genetics."<br /><br />Dr. Palmos was surprised that the study did not show an association between "classic pro-inflammatory proteins," such as C-reactive protein or interleukin-6, and severe COVID-19. "It was also surprising to see such a consistent protective effect of adhesion molecules with severe COVID-19."<br /><br />The strengths of the study included its design and use of Mendelian randomization. Weaknesses include the reliance on data from other studies, which could be subject to error despite quality control measures.<br /><br />Dr. Palmos stated, "Clinical studies in patients would need to be carried out first to confirm our findings. One such study is the COVID-Clinical Neuroscience Study, which is investigating the causes behind different aspects of COVID-19." [<a href="https://www.liverpool.ac.uk/covid-clinical-neuroscience-study/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.liverpool.ac.uk/covid-clinical-neuroscience-study/</a>]<br /><br />Dr. Youssef told MNT, "Once you start applying [the data] widely, then you understand the [true] impact." He believes that this study "adds another piece in that puzzle [...] that would inform which interventions would be offered to patients depending on their risk profile."<br /><br />[Byline: Lori Uildriks]<br /><br />--<br />Communicated by:<br />ProMED<br /><br />******<br />[2] Host genetic variation<br />Date: Mon 7 Mar 2022<br />Source: Nature [abridged, edited]<br /><a href="https://www.nature.com/articles/s41586-022-04576-6" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.nature.com/articles/s41586-022-04576-6</a><br /><br /><br />Citation<br />Kousathanas, A., Pairo-Castineira, E., Rawlik, K. et al. Whole genome sequencing reveals host factors underlying critical COVID-19. Nature (2022). Published 7 Mar 2022, doi.org/10.1038/s41586-022-04576-6<br /><br />Abstract<br />Critical COVID-19 is caused by immune-mediated inflammatory lung injury. Host genetic variation influences the development of illness requiring critical care or hospitalization following SARS-CoV-2 infection. The GenOMICC (Genetics of Mortality in Critical Care) study enables the comparison of genomes from critically-ill cases with population controls in order to find underlying disease mechanisms. Here, we use whole genome sequencing in 7491 critically-ill cases compared with 48 400 controls to discover and replicate 23 independent variants that significantly predispose to critical COVID-19. We identify 16 new independent associations, including variants within genes involved in interferon signalling (IL10RB, PLSCR1), leucocyte differentiation (BCL11A), and blood type antigen secretor status (FUT2). Using transcriptome-wide association and colocalisation to infer the effect of gene expression on disease severity, we find evidence implicating multiple genes, including reduced expression of a membrane flippase (ATP11A), and increased mucin expression (MUC1), in critical disease. Mendelian randomisation provides evidence in support of causal roles for myeloid cell adhesion molecules (SELE, ICAM5, CD209) and coagulation factor F8, all of which are potentially druggable targets. Our results are broadly consistent with a multi-component model of COVID-19 pathophysiology, in which at least 2 distinct mechanisms can predispose to life-threatening disease: failure to control viral replication, or an enhanced tendency towards pulmonary inflammation and intravascular coagulation. We show that comparison between critically-ill cases and population controls is highly efficient for detection of therapeutically-relevant mechanisms of disease.<br /><br />--<br />Communicated by:<br />Mary Marshall<br /><br />******<br />[3] Mental decline<br />Date: Wed 9 Mar 2022<br />Source: CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease Research and Policy) [edited]<br /><a href="https://www.cidrap.umn.edu/news-perspective/2022/03/mental-decline-seen-older-covid-patients-1-year-later" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.cidrap.umn.edu/news-perspective/2022/03/mental-decline-seen-older-covid-patients-1-year-later</a><br /><br /><br />Mental decline seen in older COVID patients 1 year later<br />--------------------------------------------------------<br />Cognitive impairment was more common among COVID-19 patients 60 years and older--particularly those with severe illness--released from hospitals in Wuhan, China, than among their uninfected peers, according to a 1-year follow-up study yesterday in JAMA Neurology.<br /><br />A team led by researchers at Daping Hospital in Chongqing, China, followed 1438 COVID-19 survivors aged 60 and older released from one of 3 dedicated COVID-19 hospitals in Wuhan from 10 Feb -- 10 Apr 2020, and compared them with 438 of their uninfected spouses.<br /><br />Because pre-COVID cognitive status wasn\'t available, family members provided their perceptions of cognitive changes using the Chinese version of the short form of the Informant Questionnaire on Cognitive Decline in the Elderly (IQCODE). The questionnaire contains 16 items that rate changes in memory and other domains, with decline defined as an item score of 3.5 or higher. <br /><br />Trained raters interviewed COVID-19 patients at 12 months over the phone to evaluate cognition using the Chinese version of Telephone Interview of Cognitive Status-40 (TICS-40), which includes 10 variables and has a maximum of 40 points. A score of 20 or lower was considered indicative of mild cognitive impairment (MCI), while a score of 12 or lower indicated dementia, and a decrease of 3 points or more was considered clinically meaningful cognitive decline.<br /><br />Of the COVID-19 patients. 48.1% were men, median age was 69 years, and none had a history of dementia. The control group was 50.7% men, and the median age was 67 years.<br /><br />The incidence of impaired cognition among COVID-19 survivors was 12.5% 12 months after hospital release. COVID-19 survivors had lower TICS-40 scores than controls at both 6 months (median, 29 vs 30 points) and 12 months (29 vs 31).<br /><br />Severely ill patients had lower TICS-40 scores than those with milder illness and controls (median score, 24 vs 30 vs 31) at 6 months; at 12 months, they had lower scores than controls (22.5 vs 31). Patients with mild or moderate illness and controls differed in IQCODE scores but not TICS-40 over follow-up.<br /><br />Among severely ill COVID-19 patients, 10% had dementia and 26.5% had MCI at 6 months. At 12 months, 15% had dementia and 26.2% had MCI, much higher than in those with milder illness (dementia, 0.8%; MCI, 5.4%) and controls (dementia, 0.7%; MCI, 5%). Survivors of mild or moderate COVID-19 and controls had similar incidences of dementia and MCI at both 6 and 12 months.<br /><br />Severe illness was tied to a higher risk of early-onset cognitive decline (odds ratio [OR], 4.9; 95% confidence interval [CI], 3.3 -- 7.2), late-onset cognitive decline (OR, 7.6; 95% CI, 3.6 -- 16), and progressive cognitive decline (OR, 19; 95% CI, 9.1 -- 39.5), while milder COVID-19 was associated with a higher risk of early-onset cognitive decline (OR, 1.7; 95% CI, 1.3 -- 2.3) after adjusting for age, sex, educational attainment, body mass index (BMI), and underlying illnesses.<br /><br />The prevalence of progressive decline was 21.2% with severe COVID-19, 1.2% with non-severe COVID-19, and 2.3% in controls.<br /><br />Severe COVID-19 was linked to a higher risk of cognitive impairment at 12 months (OR, 9.1; 95% CI, 5.6 -- 14.8), but milder illness was not (OR, 1.1; 95% CI, 0.7 -- 1.8), after adjusting for age, sex, educational attainment, BMI, and underlying illnesses.<br /><br />Among a subset of 438 patients and their spouses, COVID-19 survivors were older (median age, 68 vs 67 years) and had a higher incidence of hypertension (47% vs 34.5%). Both nonsevere (OR, 1.8; 95% CI, 1.1 -- 3) and severe COVID-19 (OR, 5.9; 95% CI, 3.6 -- 9.8) in this group were tied to a higher risk of cognitive impairment at 12 months, after adjustment for risk factors.<br /><br />The study authors noted that impaired cognition is common in the months after COVID-19 infection, especially among older patients. "It is worth noting that 21% of individuals with severe cases in this cohort experienced progressive cognitive decline, suggesting that COVID-19 may cause long-lasting damage to cognition," they wrote.<br /><br />"These findings imply that the pandemic may substantially contribute to the world dementia burden in the future."<br /><br />Potential factors in post-COVID cognitive decline, the authors said, include long-term low oxygen levels, chronic systemic inflammation, and direct viral invasion of the brain and resultant neuronal damage.<br /><br />"In this cohort study of COVID-19 survivors 60 years and older who were discharged from COVID-19-designated hospitals in Wuhan, China, SARS-CoV-2 infection, especially severe infection, was associated with an increase in risk of longitudinal cognitive decline," the researchers concluded. "The findings highlight the importance of immediate measures to deal with this challenge."<br /><br />[Byline: Mary Van Beusekom]<br /><br />--<br />Communicated by:<br />Mary Marshall<br /><br />[Citation. Liun YH, Chen Y, Wang QH, et al. One-Year Trajectory of Cognitive Changes in Older Survivors of COVID-19 in Wuhan, China. A Longitudinal Cohort Study. JAMA Neurol. Published online 8 Mar 2022. doi:10.1001/jamaneurol.2022.0461 <br /><br />Conclusions and relevance. In this cohort study, COVID-19 survival was associated with an increase in risk of longitudinal cognitive decline, highlighting the importance of immediate measures to deal with this challenge.<br /><br />Meaning. The findings suggest that long-term cognitive decline is common after SARS-CoV-2 infection, indicating the necessity of evaluating the impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on the future dementia burden worldwide. - Mod.LK]<br /><br />******<br />[4] Dexamethasone<br />Date: Wed 9 Mar 2022<br />Source: CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease Research and Policy) [edited]<br /><a href="https://www.cidrap.umn.edu/news-perspective/2022/03/news-scan-mar-09-2022" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.cidrap.umn.edu/news-perspective/2022/03/news-scan-mar-09-2022</a><br /><br /><br />Continuing dexamethasone after COVID hospital release shows no benefit<br />----------------------------------------------------------<br />Continuing the corticosteroid dexamethasone after hospital release didn\'t benefit COVID-19 patients who didn\'t complete the 10-day drug regimen during their stay, finds a study published yesterday [Tue 8 Mar 2022] in JAMA Network Open.<br /><br />Kaiser Permanente Southern California researchers studied the continuing use of dexamethasone at a dosage of 6mg per day after hospital release in 1164 adult COVID-19 patients who were released from 1 of 15 hospitals from [1 May to 30 Sep 2020].<br /><br />Among the 1164 patients, 59.5% continued dexamethasone after hospital release. Median patient age was 55 years, 70.6% were Hispanic, 57.9% were men, and 90% needed supplemental oxygen during hospitalization.<br /><br />The rate of hospital readmission or death within 14 days of release was 9.1% in patients who continued dexamethasone, compared with 11.4% among patients who didn\'t, but the difference wasn\'t statistically significant.<br /><br />The adjusted odds ratio (OR) for readmission or death within 14 days was 0.87 (95% confidence interval [CI], 0.58 -- 1.30) for patients who continued dexamethasone, compared with those who did not. A sensitivity analysis that limited the treatment group to those given exactly 10 days of dexamethasone showed comparable results (OR, 0.89; 95% CI, 0.55 -- 1.43).<br /><br />A subgroup analysis stratified by length of dexamethasone therapy in the hospital also yielded similar findings (1 to 3 days: OR, 0.71; 4 to 9 days: OR, 1.01), oxygen requirement at release (room air: OR, 0.91; supplemental oxygen use: OR, 0.76), and illness duration at release (10 days or less: OR, 0.81; more than 10 days: OR, 0.94).<br /><br />The study authors noted that corticosteroids like dexamethasone were the first type of medication to show a survival benefit in severely ill COVID-19 patients and are now a mainstay of hospital treatment. Because early readmissions after hospital release are largely due to respiratory causes that probably reflect illness progression, the authors had theorized that continuing corticosteroids could reduce these outcomes.<br /><br />"Continuing treatment with dexamethasone, 6mg/d, at discharge was not associated with a reduction in 14-day all-cause readmission or mortality," the researchers wrote. "This finding suggests that dexamethasone should not be routinely prescribed beyond discharge."<br /><br />--<br />Communicated by:<br />Mary Marshall<br />&lt;mjm2020@googlemail.com><br /><br />[Citation. Huang CW, Yu AS, Song H,et al. Association Between Dexamethasone Treatment After Hospital Discharge for Patients With COVID-19 Infection and Rates of Hospital Readmission and Mortality. JAMA Netw Open. 2022;5(3):e221455. doi:10.1001/jamanetworkopen.2022.1455<br /><br />Introduction. Corticosteroids were the first medication class to demonstrate mortality benefit among patients with COVID-19 and are now a cornerstone of COVID-19 treatment. In the Randomized Evaluation of COVID-19 Therapy (RECOVERY) trial, the use of dexamethasone, 6 mg/d, up to 10 days or until discharge resulted in lower mortality. However, with no standardized discharge practices, corticosteroid prescription practices at discharge have varied.<br /><br />Conclusions and relevance. In this cohort study of patients with COVID-19, continuing treatment with dexamethasone, 6mg/d, at discharge was not associated with a reduction in 14-day all-cause readmission or mortality. This finding suggests that dexamethasone should not be routinely prescribed beyond discharge for individuals with COVID-19. - Mod.LK]<br /><br />********<br />[5] WHO: Daily new cases reported (as of 9 Mar 2022)<br />Date: Wed 9 Mar 2022<br />Source: WHO [abridged, edited]<br /><a href="https://covid19.who.int/table" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://covid19.who.int/table</a><br /><br /><br />*Daily case reports as of 9 Mar 2022 16:48 CET<br /><br />Surveillance<br />------------<br />WHO region (no. countries/territories):<br />Total confirmed cases (new cases in last 24 hours) / Total deaths (new deaths in last 24 hours)<br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------------<br />Western Pacific Region (19): 29 262 597 (730 009) / 189 656 (892)<br />European Region (61): 184 618 493 (777 549) / 1 894 846 (2509)<br />South East Asia Region (10): 56 285 919 (55 423) / 767 165 (533)<br />Eastern Mediterranean Region (22): 21 362 890 (14 737) / 337 357 (253)<br />Region of the Americas (54): 148 322 100 (125 821) / 2 652 042 (2289)<br />African Region (49): 8 460 530 (669) / 170 403 (11)<br />Cases on an international conveyance: 764 (0) / 13 (0)<br /><br />Confirmed cases (new cases in last 24 hours) / Total deaths (new deaths in last 24 hours)<br />Grand total: 448 313 293 (1 704 208) / 6 011 482 (6487)<br /><br />--<br />Communicated by:<br />ProMED<br /><br />[Data by country, area, or territory for 9 Mar 2022 can be accessed at <br /><a href="https://promedmail.org/wp-content/uploads/world-pdf/2022%20WHO%20Data%20Tables%20MAR9_1646937489.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://promedmail.org/wp-content/uploads/world-pdf/2022%20WHO%20Data%20Tables%20MAR9_1646937489.pdf</a>.<br /><br />- The Americas region reported 7.3% of cases and 35.2% of deaths in the past 24 hours having reported more than 148.32 million cases, 2nd to the European region, as the most severely affected region. Since the potentially ", 'alert_tag_id': None, 'dup_count': '0', 'dup_of': None, 'unique_string': '20220311.8701896', 'info_hash': None, 'submitted_by': '3419', 'reviewed': None, 'search_string_id': None, 'content': 'JAPANESE ENCEPHALITIS - AUSTRALIA (06): (QUEENSLAND, SOUTH AUSTRALIA) PIG\n*************************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nIn this update:\r\n[1] Queensland: pigs\r\n[2] South Australia: pigs\r\n\r\n******\r\n[1] Queensland: pigs\r\nDate: Fri 4 Mar 2021 15:00 AEST\r\nSource: Queensland Country Life [edited]\r\n<https://www.queenslandcountrylife.com.au/story/7644999/pork-giant-confirms-japanese-encephalitis-cases-at-piggeries/>\r\n\r\n\r\nAustralia\'s biggest pork producer, SunPork, has confirmed its piggeries have been impacted by Japanese encephalitis virus (JEV). JEV is a mosquito-borne viral disease that is spread through the bite of an infected mosquito to people and animals -- mostly pigs and horses.\r\n\r\nSunPork Group CEO and managing director Dr Robert van Barneveld said several of their sites in the eastern states had been impacted and they were working through it. "One of the Goondiwindi farms has been infected...we have an infected premises in New South Wales.... There are infected premises all down the east coast," Dr van Barneveld said. "We\'ve been working on this endlessly since Friday night [25 Feb 2022] when we first were made aware that it was an issue."\r\n\r\nDr van Barneveld said it was not a new occurrence and there was no cause for alarm in the pork industry. "The piggeries are innocent bystanders. Pigs are not going to infect other pigs. It\'s not going to have a negative effect at an abattoir. The pork is safe to eat. It\'s play on," he said. "If you\'re going to have an emergency animal disease, this is the one to have. We\'re not actually anticipating any significant disruption to our operations at all."\r\n\r\nPigs are the focus from a human health perspective, as they can infect mosquitoes that can then infect humans. This is not the case with horses.\r\n\r\nHumans can become infected with JEV through the bite of an infected mosquito. The virus cannot be transmitted between humans, and it cannot be caught through eating pork or pig products.\r\n\r\n"If a mosquito bites people and if it bites horses, they\'re called dead-end hosts, so it doesn\'t pass on. If it bites a pig -- a pig\'s an amplifier -- so if another mosquito bites that pig while it\'s viremic, which is a very short period of time, it can transmit in the mosquito population," Dr van Barneveld said. "That\'s why there are controls around the movement of pigs."\r\n\r\nDr van Barneveld said authorities were working well together to protect humans and animals. "This is about protecting people and the bad guy in the process is a mosquito and the weather patterns," he said. "The state health departments and the state chief veterinary officers have worked extremely well together. They understand that eradicating it is going to be very unlikely and they\'re being very practical around the movement of pigs and making sure people are safe."\r\n\r\nHe said SunPork had done a lot of preparedness work around African swine fever and had good relationships with the state chief veterinary officers which puts it in a very good position to manage this. "When you get a disease like this, there are a whole stack of legislative instruments that are pushed into place that have to be executed, and they\'re following those processes. Everyone\'s fairly calm about it and [I] think we can manage through it with minimal disruption."\r\n\r\nA vaccine for horses and pigs is available in countries where the disease is endemic, but no JE vaccines for animals are registered for use in Australia, and there is no effective treatment for JE in animals.\r\n\r\nOn Friday [4 Mar 2022], Australia\'s Chief Veterinary Officer Dr Mark Schipp said JEV had been confirmed at 14 piggeries across NSW, SA, Queensland and Victoria. This follows a confirmed human case of JEV in Queensland. Queensland Health said the confirmed case had recent travel in regional southern Queensland and is currently being treated in a Brisbane hospital.\r\n\r\nLess than 1% of people infected may develop a serious illness such as encephalitis and experience symptoms including neck stiffness, severe headache and coma, and more rarely, permanent neurological complications or death.\r\n\r\nGoondiwindi and District Veterinary Services owner and vet Dr Trisha Pullos said the recent heavy rain in parts of Queensland and NSW had brought with it more mosquitoes and potentially more risk of JEV. "We\'ve had quite a bit of rain like everybody else, and the mozzies out here are brutal," Dr Pullos said.\r\n\r\nDr Pullos said the main issues were observed in piglets -- pigs didn\'t seem to be affected physically. "If the piglets are born dead or weak or malformed or something really unusual or if the sow aborts the whole litter -- that\'s the 1st sign," she said. "Pigs can amplify the virus. It\'s not transferable between humans; it seems to be just the mosquitoes that are how we can catch it, so if you can keep the mozzies off you, then we\'ll be a little bit safer."\r\n\r\nShe said it was important to get information from reliable sources and anyone who was worried should contact their vet. "Anybody who\'s got 2 pigs in the backyard, or a pig for the kids, or a little kill herd at the back of the property -- they need to contact their local vet and just say, \'Hey, I\'ve got 2 pigs here. What do I need to keep an eye out for?\' Then what we\'ll do is we\'ll let our district vet know who will then let the Chief Veterinary Officer know so we can try and track it a lot faster, because if someone goes and tells their neighbour but doesn\'t tell the vet then we\'re already behind the 8 ball."\r\n\r\nShe said there was a risk people had "bad news fatigue" and wouldn\'t take things seriously. "I\'ve found that everyone\'s a little bit worn out. There\'s been quite a few comments on our Facebook posts where people are just like, \'Not again\', or \'What now?\' \'Can it just end?\' We might be at risk of going, \'Oh, whatever\', and I want to make sure that they\'re not going to do that."\r\n\r\nJEV was detected in 3 people in the Torres Strait Islands in 1995. In 1998, 2 further cases were detected in people in Queensland, including one acquired on the Australian mainland.\r\n\r\nJEV is mostly transmitted by bites from infected mosquito vectors and is maintained in mosquito-waterbird or mosquito-waterbird-pig cycles. Waterbirds, particularly wading birds such as herons and egrets, are the main source from which transmission of JEV can occur. They are important amplifying hosts and hold enough virus in their blood to infect vectors for one to 7 days.\r\n\r\nPigs are also major amplifiers of the virus and develop levels of virus in the blood sufficient to infect vectors for around 4 days. Horses and a wide range of other species may be infected but do not develop levels of virus in the blood sufficient to infect mosquitoes, so are not involved in maintaining disease in an area.\r\n\r\n[Byline: Brandon Long]\r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nProMED\r\n\r\n******\r\n[2] South Australia: pigs\r\nDate: Sun 6 Mar 2022 23:58 ACDT\r\nSource: ABC News [edited]\r\n<https://www.abc.net.au/news/rural/2022-03-07/japanese-encephalitis-outbreak-reaches-sa/100888042>\r\n\r\n\r\nKey points\r\n----------\r\n- The virus has been detected at 21 piggeries, 3 human cases have been confirmed, and the federal government predicts more cases will be detected.\r\n- The Chief Veterinary Officer says it\'s unusual to detect Japanese encephalitis (JE) so far south.\r\n- Pork SA [South Australia] says piggeries will still be able to operate, but restrictions are in place.\r\n\r\nThe outbreak of Japanese encephalitis (JE) is now impacting 4 states as public health officials work to contain it. Since the potentially fatal mosquito-borne virus was first detected near the New South Wales-Victoria border last week, cases have been recorded in Queensland and South Australia.\r\n\r\nA Department of Agriculture, Water and the Environment spokesperson said 3 cases of JE had been confirmed in humans, and a number of possible cases were under investigation. The spokesperson said it was anticipated that more cases would be confirmed shortly.\r\n\r\nAustralia\'s Chief Veterinary Officer Mark Schipp said animals at 21 piggeries had been infected. "We\'re mapping the presence of waterbirds, feral pigs or piggeries and people that have contracted the infection," he said. "We\'re undertaking mosquito surveillance and mosquito control on those farms."\r\n\r\nThere is a vaccine available as well. "That\'s the purpose of the mapping that we\'re undertaking at the moment -- to prioritise those vaccinations and then rolling the vaccinations out further from there," Mr Schipp said. The movement of pigs on farms with positive cases is being restricted.\r\n\r\nPork SA chairman Andrew Johnson is urging pork producers to report suspected cases. "Farmers that see any symptoms, they shouldn\'t be afraid to advise accordingly," he said.\r\n\r\nJE cannot be transmitted to humans by eating pork products and producers will still be able to transport livestock to abattoirs. "If there\'s a suspect case, or if there is a positive case, those farms will be issued a movement permit that will allow them to continue business," Mr Johnson said.\r\n\r\nThe virus is transmitted by mosquitoes and cannot be passed from livestock to humans. "Japanese encephalitis is a virus carried by mosquitoes from water birds or pigs to humans or horses," Mr Schipp said. "Pigs are an amplifying host, so they produce a lot of virus and the mosquitoes pick that up and carry it forward into humans and horses."\r\n\r\nJE is not normally found in Australia and health officials survey the Torres Strait and the Top End every year to check for outbreaks. This outbreak has been found along the Murray River corridor.\r\n\r\nMr Schipp said it was surprising to find JE so far south. "It will have a large impact on human health and industry," he said. "The impacts on tourism, for example, could be significant. We\'re doing everything we can to try and minimise its spread and ensure that people are vaccinated."\r\n\r\n[Byline: Megan Hughes]\r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nProMED\n\n[Pigs have been effective sentinels for the presence of Japanese encephalitis virus (JEV) in the 4 affected Australian states. The above reports provide a comprehensive overview of the current JEV situation in the 4 states. There has been no indication in the above or previous reports that ProMED has received about the frequency of aborted litters or teratogenic effects in the affected piggeries. Restriction on movement of pigs out of infected piggeries could be a problem for producers. The Australian response is a good example of implementation of a One Health approach to an unexpected disease outbreak. Public health and animal health officials have been working closely together on the outbreak and informing the public in a way that promotes concern and reduces risk of exposure without panic. - Mod.TY\r\n\r\nIn adult swine reproductive failure may be the only clinical sign while piglets may suffer from CNS disease, unthriftiness, etc. For more information on JEV in swine see <https://vetmed.iastate.edu/vdpam/FSVD/swine/index-diseases/japanese-B-encephalitis>. - Australia (06): (QL, SA) pig <br /><span class="blue">Archive Number:</span> 20220311.8701896<div class="text1">JAPANESE ENCEPHALITIS - AUSTRALIA (06): (QUEENSLAND, SOUTH AUSTRALIA) PIG<br />*************************************************************************<br />A ProMED-mail post<br /><a href="http://www.promedmail.org" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">http://www.promedmail.org</a><br />ProMED-mail is a program of the<br />International Society for Infectious Diseases<br /><a href="http://www.isid.org" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">http://www.isid.org</a><br /><br />In this update:<br />[1] Queensland: pigs<br />[2] South Australia: pigs<br /><br />******<br />[1] Queensland: pigs<br />Date: Fri 4 Mar 2021 15:00 AEST<br />Source: Queensland Country Life [edited]<br /><a href="https://www.queenslandcountrylife.com.au/story/7644999/pork-giant-confirms-japanese-encephalitis-cases-at-piggeries/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.queenslandcountrylife.com.au/story/7644999/pork-giant-confirms-japanese-encephalitis-cases-at-piggeries/</a><br /><br /><br />Australia\'s biggest pork producer, SunPork, has confirmed its piggeries have been impacted by Japanese encephalitis virus (JEV). JEV is a mosquito-borne viral disease that is spread through the bite of an infected mosquito to people and animals -- mostly pigs and horses.<br /><br />SunPork Group CEO and managing director Dr Robert van Barneveld said several of their sites in the eastern states had been impacted and they were working through it. "One of the Goondiwindi farms has been infected...we have an infected premises in New South Wales.... There are infected premises all down the east coast," Dr van Barneveld said. "We\'ve been working on this endlessly since Friday night [25 Feb 2022] when we first were made aware that it was an issue."<br /><br />Dr van Barneveld said it was not a new occurrence and there was no cause for alarm in the pork industry. "The piggeries are innocent bystanders. Pigs are not going to infect other pigs. It\'s not going to have a negative effect at an abattoir. The pork is safe to eat. It\'s play on," he said. "If you\'re going to have an emergency animal disease, this is the one to have. We\'re not actually anticipating any significant disruption to our operations at all."<br /><br />Pigs are the focus from a human health perspective, as they can infect mosquitoes that can then infect humans. This is not the case with horses.<br /><br />Humans can become infected with JEV through the bite of an infected mosquito. The virus cannot be transmitted between humans, and it cannot be caught through eating pork or pig products.<br /><br />"If a mosquito bites people and if it bites horses, they\'re called dead-end hosts, so it doesn\'t pass on. If it bites a pig -- a pig\'s an amplifier -- so if another mosquito bites that pig while it\'s viremic, which is a very short period of time, it can transmit in the mosquito population," Dr van Barneveld said. "That\'s why there are controls around the movement of pigs."<br /><br />Dr van Barneveld said authorities were working well together to protect humans and animals. "This is about protecting people and the bad guy in the process is a mosquito and the weather patterns," he said. "The state health departments and the state chief veterinary officers have worked extremely well together. They understand that eradicating it is going to be very unlikely and they\'re being very practical around the movement of pigs and making sure people are safe."<br /><br />He said SunPork had done a lot of preparedness work around African swine fever and had good relationships with the state chief veterinary officers which puts it in a very good position to manage this. "When you get a disease like this, there are a whole stack of legislative instruments that are pushed into place that have to be executed, and they\'re following those processes. Everyone\'s fairly calm about it and [I] think we can manage through it with minimal disruption."<br /><br />A vaccine for horses and pigs is available in countries where the disease is endemic, but no JE vaccines for animals are registered for use in Australia, and there is no effective treatment for JE in animals.<br /><br />On Friday [4 Mar 2022], Australia\'s Chief Veterinary Officer Dr Mark Schipp said JEV had been confirmed at 14 piggeries across NSW, SA, Queensland and Victoria. This follows a confirmed human case of JEV in Queensland. Queensland Health said the confirmed case had recent travel in regional southern Queensland and is currently being treated in a Brisbane hospital.<br /><br />Less than 1% of people infected may develop a serious illness such as encephalitis and experience symptoms including neck stiffness, severe headache and coma, and more rarely, permanent neurological complications or death.<br /><br />Goondiwindi and District Veterinary Services owner and vet Dr Trisha Pullos said the recent heavy rain in parts of Queensland and NSW had brought with it more mosquitoes and potentially more risk of JEV. "We\'ve had quite a bit of rain like everybody else, and the mozzies out here are brutal," Dr Pullos said.<br /><br />Dr Pullos said the main issues were observed in piglets -- pigs didn\'t seem to be affected physically. "If the piglets are born dead or weak or malformed or something really unusual or if the sow aborts the whole litter -- that\'s the 1st sign," she said. "Pigs can amplify the virus. It\'s not transferable between humans; it seems to be just the mosquitoes that are how we can catch it, so if you can keep the mozzies off you, then we\'ll be a little bit safer."<br /><br />She said it was important to get information from reliable sources and anyone who was worried should contact their vet. "Anybody who\'s got 2 pigs in the backyard, or a pig for the kids, or a little kill herd at the back of the property -- they need to contact their local vet and just say, \'Hey, I\'ve got 2 pigs here. What do I need to keep an eye out for?\' Then what we\'ll do is we\'ll let our district vet know who will then let the Chief Veterinary Officer know so we can try and track it a lot faster, because if someone goes and tells their neighbour but doesn\'t tell the vet then we\'re already behind the 8 ball."<br /><br />She said there was a risk people had "bad news fatigue" and wouldn\'t take things seriously. "I\'ve found that everyone\'s a little bit worn out. There\'s been quite a few comments on our Facebook posts where people are just like, \'Not again\', or \'What now?\' \'Can it just end?\' We might be at risk of going, \'Oh, whatever\', and I want to make sure that they\'re not going to do that."<br /><br />JEV was detected in 3 people in the Torres Strait Islands in 1995. In 1998, 2 further cases were detected in people in Queensland, including one acquired on the Australian mainland.<br /><br />JEV is mostly transmitted by bites from infected mosquito vectors and is maintained in mosquito-waterbird or mosquito-waterbird-pig cycles. Waterbirds, particularly wading birds such as herons and egrets, are the main source from which transmission of JEV can occur. They are important amplifying hosts and hold enough virus in their blood to infect vectors for one to 7 days.<br /><br />Pigs are also major amplifiers of the virus and develop levels of virus in the blood sufficient to infect vectors for around 4 days. Horses and a wide range of other species may be infected but do not develop levels of virus in the blood sufficient to infect mosquitoes, so are not involved in maintaining disease in an area.<br /><br />[Byline: Brandon Long]<br /><br />--<br />Communicated by:<br />ProMED<br /><br />******<br />[2] South Australia: pigs<br />Date: Sun 6 Mar 2022 23:58 ACDT<br />Source: ABC News [edited]<br /><a href="https://www.abc.net.au/news/rural/2022-03-07/japanese-encephalitis-outbreak-reaches-sa/100888042" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.abc.net.au/news/rural/2022-03-07/japanese-encephalitis-outbreak-reaches-sa/100888042</a><br /><br /><br />Key points<br />----------<br />- The virus has been detected at 21 piggeries, 3 human cases have been confirmed, and the federal government predicts more cases will be detected.<br />- The Chief Veterinary Officer says it\'s unusual to detect Japanese encephalitis (JE) so far south.<br />- Pork SA [South Australia] says piggeries will still be able to operate, but restrictions are in place.<br /><br />The outbreak of Japanese encephalitis (JE) is now impacting 4 states as public health officials work to contain it. Since the potentially fatal mosquito-borne virus was first detected near the New South Wales-Victoria border last week, cases have been recorded in Queensland and South Australia.<br /><br />A Department of Agriculture, Water and the Environment spokesperson said 3 cases of JE had been confirmed in humans, and a number of possible cases were under investigation. The spokesperson said it was anticipated that more cases would be confirmed shortly.<br /><br />Australia\'s Chief Veterinary Officer Mark Schipp said animals at 21 piggeries had been infected. "We\'re mapping the presence of waterbirds, feral pigs or piggeries and people that have contracted the infection," he said. "We\'re undertaking mosquito surveillance and mosquito control on those farms."<br /><br />There is a vaccine available as well. "That\'s the purpose of the mapping that we\'re undertaking at the moment -- to prioritise those vaccinations and then rolling the vaccinations out further from there," Mr Schipp said. The movement of pigs on farms with positive cases is being restricted.<br /><br />Pork SA chairman Andrew Johnson is urging pork producers to report suspected cases. "Farmers that see any symptoms, they shouldn\'t be afraid to advise accordingly," he said.<br /><br />JE cannot be transmitted to humans by eating pork products and producers will still be able to transport livestock to abattoirs. "If there\'s a suspect case, or if there is a positive case, those farms will be issued a movement permit that will allow them to continue business," Mr Johnson said.<br /><br />The virus is transmitted by mosquitoes and cannot be passed from livestock to humans. "Japanese encephalitis is a virus carried by mosquitoes from water birds or pigs to humans or horses," Mr Schipp said. "Pigs are an amplifying host, so they produce a lot of virus and the mosquitoes pick that up and carry it forward into humans and horses."<br /><br />JE is not normally found in Australia and health officials survey the Torres Strait and the Top End every year to check for outbreaks. This outbreak has been found along the Murray River corridor.<br /><br />Mr Schipp said it was surprising to find JE so far south. "It will have a large impact on human health and industry," he said. "The impacts on tourism, for example, could be significant. We\'re doing everything we can to try and minimise its spread and ensure that people are vaccinated."<br /><br />[Byline: Megan Hughes]<br /><br />--<br />Communicated by:<br />ProMED<br /><br />[Pigs have been effective sentinels for the presence of Japanese encephalitis virus (JEV) in the 4 affected Australian states. The above reports provide a comprehensive overview of the current JEV situation in the 4 states. There has been no indication in the above or previous reports that ProMED has received about the frequency of aborted litters or teratogenic effects in the affected piggeries. Restriction on movement of pigs out of infected piggeries could be a problem for producers. The Australian response is a good example of implementation of a One Health approach to an unexpected disease outbreak. Public health and animal health officials have been working closely together on the outbreak and informing the public in a way that promotes concern and reduces risk of exposure without panic. - Mod.TY<br /><br />In adult swine reproductive failure may be the only clinical sign while piglets may suffer from CNS disease, unthriftiness, etc. For more information on JEV in swine see <a href="https://vetmed.iastate.edu/vdpam/FSVD/swine/index-diseases/japanese-B-encephalitis" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://vetmed.iastate.edu/vdpam/FSVD/swine/index-diseases/japanese-B-encephalitis</a>. - PHILIPPINES: (PANGASINAN) POULTRY, DIFFERENTIAL DIAGNOSIS\n*****************************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Wed 9 Mar 2022\r\nSource: Rappler [edited]\r\n<https://www.rappler.com/nation/newscastle-diseas-not-avian-flu-killing-chickens-pangasinan/>\r\n\r\n\r\nThe deaths of hundreds of chickens in various barangays in Labrador town, Pangasinan have raised fears of a possible Avian Influenza outbreak in the area.\r\n\r\nBut an investigation by the Labrador local government of the chicken deaths in Barangay Magsaysay led to discovery of a Newcastle Disease (ND) outbreak, not the Asian Avian Influenza (H5N1).\r\n\r\nDr. Joel Abalos, municipal veterinarian (OMVet) for Labrador town said they received reports from Barangay Magsaysay on [4 Mar 2022].\r\n\r\nHis office immediately took blood and swab samples and sent these to the Regional Animal Disease Diagnostic Laboratory in Sta. Barbara town.\r\n\r\nHe said the results, which came in on Monday [7 Mar 2022], confirmed the presence of Newcastle Disease.\r\n\r\nAbalos said 600 other chickens have since died in big poultry farms in Barangays Bolo, Gonzalo, Poblacion, and Dulig. Abalos said the number does not yet include backyard poultry farms. He said the losses amount to P120 000 [USD 2297].\r\n\r\nBoth ND and Avian Flu cause high mortality and drastic drop in egg production, Abalos said. But ND does not affect humans, unlike Avian Flu.\r\n\r\nThe OMVet said they would no longer consider culling the chickens as disinfection and vaccination can control outbreaks.\r\n\r\nThe OMVet will conduct a mass [ND] vaccination on Thursday [10 Mar 2022], in affected Labrador town poultry farms.\r\n\r\nAbalos said the ND could have been brought about by migratory birds, while hinting that local transmission was also possible.\r\n\r\nAbalos urged poultry farms and backyard raisers to vaccinate their chickens annually [against ND] to prevent losses. \r\n\r\n[Byline: Ahikam Pasion] \r\n\r\n--\r\nCommunicated by:\r\nProMED\n\n[Further information about the diagnosis of ND and about its vaccine production and testing methods, is available in OIE's Terrestrial Manual, at <https://www.oie.int/fileadmin/Home/eng/Health_standards/tahm/3.03.14_NEWCASTLE_DIS.pdf> (chapter updated 2021). -
